Reciting from the Mushaf in Prayer: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab ruling on mushaf in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on reciting from a mushaf during prayer and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Reciting from a mushaf invalidates prayer according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, despite the Sahibayn's contrary opinion.

Reading the Quran during prayer is a core act, but what if you read from a physical copy (mushaf)? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute, with Abu Hanifa holding the prayer invalid due to excessive action, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it valid but disliked. Let us dissect the proofs and understand the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the position of Abu Hanifa (invalidates), the Sahibayn (valid but makruh), and the stronger opinion (invalidates in all cases).
The stronger opinion is the mufta bihi.
2
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: carrying and turning pages is 'amal kathir. The Sahibayn's proof: looking at the mushaf is an act of worship. The stronger opinion: reading from mushaf is not an action of prayer.
The stronger view equates carried and fixed mushaf.
3
Distinguish the conditions
Note when the prayer is not invalidated: according to Abu Hanifa, if the mushaf is fixed and not handled; according to the stronger view, if the sight falls accidentally without understanding.
These conditions show the flexibility in the school.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: a person who recites from mushaf must repeat the prayer according to the mufta bihi, while Abu Yusuf would consider it valid but disliked.
The mufta bihi is the operative ruling for fatwa.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i school
The Shafi'i school allows reciting from mushaf without dislike, showing a clear cross-madhhab difference.
Understanding ikhtilaf across madhahib is key to fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مصحف [عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا لا تفسد؛ لأن النظر إليه عبادة، ولكنه يكره؛ لأنه تشبه بأهل الكتاب. (ع)] أي عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن حمل المصحف وتقليب الأوراق والنظر فيه عمل كثير، فعلى هذا لو كان موضوعاً بين يديه وهو لا يحمل ولا يقلب أو قرأ المكتوب في المحراب لا تفسد، وعلل بعضهم بأن التلقن من المصحف تعلّم ليس من أعمال الصلاة، وهذا يوجب التسوية بين المحمول وغيره فيفسد بكل حال، وهو الصحيح، ولا فرق بين ما إذا قرأ قليلاً أو كثيرا، وقيل: إذا قرأ آية تامة تفسد وإلا فلا وهذا كله إذا أبصره متفهما، أما لو وقع بصره على المصحف لا تفسد، وعند الشافعي: يجزئه بغير كراهة. [رمز الحقائق: ٧٤/١]
Mushaf [according to Abu Hanifa invalidates, and they both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it does not invalidate, because looking at it is worship, but it is disliked because it resembles the People of the Book. (Ayni)] i.e. according to Abu Hanifa, because carrying the mushaf, turning pages, and looking at it is a lot of action, so if it is placed in front of him and he does not carry or turn pages, or reads what is written in the mihrab, it does not invalidate. And some have reasoned that learning from the mushaf is not from the actions of prayer, and this necessitates equivalence between carried and other, so it invalidates in any case, and that is correct. There is no difference between reading a little or a lot. And it is said: if he reads one complete verse it invalidates, otherwise not. All this if he sees it with understanding; if his sight falls on the mushaf accidentally, it does not invalidate. And according to al-Shafi'i: it is permissible without dislike. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 74/1]
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling of reciting from a written Quran (mushaf) during prayer. Abu Hanifa rules that it invalidates the prayer because the actions of holding, turning pages, and looking constitute 'amal kathir (excessive movement) which is not permissible in prayer. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn) hold that it does not invalidate because looking at the mushaf is a form of worship, but they still consider it makruh (disliked) due to resemblance to the People of the Book (Christians and Jews who use scripture in worship). The text then notes a stronger opinion (attributed to some scholars) that since reciting from the mushaf is not an essential action of prayer, it invalidates in all cases, and this is considered the correct (sahih) view. Details: if the mushaf is placed without carrying/turning, it does not invalidate per Abu Hanifa; the stronger opinion equates all cases. The ruling also depends on whether the person is understanding what he reads; accidental sight does not invalidate. The Shafi'i school allows it without dislike. The relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that reciting from the mushaf invalidates the prayer (as per the 'sahih' view).

Key Terms
مصحف (mushaf): A physical copy of the Quran in codex form.
عمل كثير (amal kathir): Excessive action that is not part of prayer, causing invalidation according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on reciting from the mushaf during prayer?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid but disliked.
C) The prayer is valid if only one verse is read.
D) The prayer is valid if the mushaf is fixed without being held.

Key Takeaway
Reciting from a mushaf during prayer invalidates the prayer according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, due to 'amal kathir and the act not being part of prayer.

Eating and Drinking in Prayer: Invalidation and Exceptions
Ruling that eating and drinking intentionally or forgetfully invalidate prayer with threshold exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating and drinking during prayer, identify the threshold for invalidation, and distinguish between internal and external consumption.

Core Idea
Eating or drinking any amount, intentionally or forgetfully, invalidates the prayer because it contradicts the state of worship.

The Hanafi school takes a strict view on any intake during prayer. This lesson examines the ruling that even a small amount, if taken from outside, breaks the prayer, along with exceptions for residual matter and pre-existing sweetness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Eating or drinking during prayer invalidates the prayer (batil).
2
Recognize the basis
It is considered 'amal kathir (excessive action) and contradicts the state of prayer.
3
Note the exceptions
Swallowing residual matter from between teeth does not invalidate if it did not fill the mouth. Swallowing the sweetness of sugar already in the mouth before prayer does not invalidate.
4
Understand the threshold
If the consumed item is from inside the mouth (e.g., food remnants), it must be the size of a chickpea (himmasa) or more to invalidate. If taken from outside, even a sesame seed (samisma) invalidates.
5
Contrast with fasting
Forgetful eating in prayer invalidates, whereas forgetful eating in fasting does not break the fast, because prayer is a reminding state (hala mudhakkara) unlike fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكله وشربه [عامداً أو ناسياً قليلاً أو كثيراً؛ لأنه عمل كثير. (ع، ط لأنه ينافي الصلاة، ولو ابتلع ما خرج من بين أسنانه لم تفسد صلاته إذا لم يكن ملأ الفم، ولو شرع في الصلاة وفي فمه شيء من السكر فابتلع ذوبه فإنها تفسد ولو بدون مضغ، بخلاف ما لو أكل سكراً قبل الشروع ثم شرع فوجد حلاوته في فمه وابتلعها، فإنه لا تفسد، بخلاف الأكل ناسياً في الصوم؛ لأن الصلاة حالة مذكرة بخلاف الصوم، والمراد بالقليل المفسد ما بلغ قدر الحمصة فصاعداً، لا ما دون ذلك، إلا أن يأخذه من الخارج فيفسد ولو قدر سمسمة، وبقي من المفسدات: الموت والارتداد بالقلب والجنون والإغماء وكل ما أوجب الوضوء أو الغسل وترك الركن بلا قضاء أو الشرط بلا عذر وزلة القاري وحكي عن أبي القاسم الصفار أن الصلاة إذا جازت من وجه فسدت من وجه يحكم بالفساد احتياطاً إلا في باب القراءة؛ لأن للناس فيها عموم البلوى
And his eating and drinking [intentionally or forgetfully, little or much, because it is a lot of action. (Ayni, Taha) because it contradicts prayer. And if he swallows what comes out from between his teeth, his prayer is not invalidated if it did not fill the mouth. And if he begins prayer and in his mouth is some sugar, and he swallows its melted part, it invalidates even without chewing, contrary to if he ate sugar before starting then started and found its sweetness in his mouth and swallowed it, then it does not invalidate. This is contrary to eating forgetfully in fasting, because prayer is a reminding state unlike fasting. And what is meant by little that invalidates is what reaches the size of a chickpea upward, not less than that, unless he takes it from outside, then it invalidates even if the size of a sesame seed. And remaining from the invalidators: death, apostasy in heart, insanity, fainting, and everything that necessitates wudu or ghusl, and leaving a rukn without making up, or a condition without excuse, and the slip of a reciter. And it is narrated from Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar that if prayer is valid from one aspect and invalid from another, it is judged invalid as a precaution, except in the chapter of recitation because there is general affliction for people.]
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This ruling covers eating and drinking during prayer. The Hanafi rule is that any intake of food or drink, whether intentional or forgetful, little or much, invalidates the prayer because it contradicts the state of prayer and constitutes 'amal kathir. The text provides several exceptions and details: swallowing residue from between teeth does not invalidate if it did not fill the mouth; swallowing sugar that was already in the mouth before prayer begins (e.g., its sweetness) does not invalidate, but if one starts prayer with sugar in the mouth and then deliberately swallows it, it does invalidate. The threshold for 'little' that invalidates is the size of a chickpea (himmasa); less than that does not invalidate unless it is taken from outside, in which case even a sesame seed invalidates. The text also lists other invalidators: death, apostasy (in heart), insanity, fainting, anything that requires wudu or ghusl, leaving a pillar or condition without excuse, and a reciter's slip. It mentions a principle from Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar: if a prayer is valid from one aspect but invalid from another, it is judged invalid for precaution, except in matters of recitation due to general affliction (i.e., made easy).

Key Terms
الحمصة (al-himmasa): A chickpea; used as a measure for the minimum amount of food that invalidates prayer (if from inside).
عمل كثير (amal kathir): Excessive action that invalidates prayer; eating/drinking is automatically kathir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does eating a small amount (e.g., half a chickpea) forgetfully during prayer invalidate it?
A) Yes, it invalidates because any eating is 'amal kathir.
B) No, because it is forgetful and the amount is small.
C) Yes, only if the amount fills the mouth.
D) No, because it is considered similar to fasting.

Key Takeaway
Eating or drinking any amount during prayer, whether intentionally or forgetfully, invalidates the prayer, with exceptions for residual matter between teeth and pre-existing sweetness, following the internal/external threshold.

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Abath, Fidgeting, and Posture
Listing of disliked acts in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four disliked actions mentioned in the text and explain why each is considered makruh tanzihi.

Core Idea
Unnecessary movements in prayer reduce reward without invalidating it.

Prayer is a state of humble devotion before Allah. The Hanafi school identifies certain actions that, while not breaking the prayer, diminish its perfection and reward. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lists four such makruhat—commonly misunderstood as minor or harmless. Let's parse them precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four disliked actions
From the Arabic text, extract each action: playing with garment/body (عبث), turning pebbles (قلب الحصى) except once for prostration, cracking fingers (فرقعة الأصابع), and placing hands on hips (التخصر).
Note the exception for turning pebbles once.
2
Understand the legal category
These actions are ruled makruh tanzihi—disliked but not sinful. They do not invalidate prayer but contradict its spirit of khushu'.
Compare with makruh tahrimi which is closer to haram; here it's lighter.
3
Recognize the underlying rationale
Each action either resembles heedless behavior (abath), mimics disbelievers (takhassur: 'posture of people of Hell'), or distracts from prayer (cracking fingers). The exception for turning pebbles once shows necessity permits minor movement.
The hadith on takhassur is evidence for its dislike.
4
Apply the ruling to modern scenarios
Modern equivalents: fidgeting with a phone, adjusting glasses repeatedly, or cracking knuckles. These fall under the same dislike unless for a valid need (e.g., wiping sweat).
The principle: avoid unnecessary movement, but don't become obsessive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره عبثه بثوبه وبدنه، وقلب الحصى إلا للسجود مرة، وفرقعة الأصابع، والتخصر
And it is disliked: his playing with his garment and his body, and turning over pebbles except for prostration once, and cracking the fingers, and placing hands on hips (takhassur).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage enumerates several makruh (disliked) actions during prayer. These are not invalidating but reduce the reward. The actions include: 1) 'Abath' (playing) with one's clothing or body without necessity; 2) Smoothing pebbles on the ground (if needed to perform sajdah, it is allowed once); 3) Cracking fingers (farqa' al-asabi'); 4) Placing hands on the hips (takhassur). The ruling is that these are makruh tahrimi or tanzihi depending on the specific act. The source for the disapproval includes hadith. The passage sets the standard for respectful demeanor in prayer.

Key Terms
عبث (abath): Unnecessary fidgeting or playing with one's clothes or body during prayer.
فرقعة الأصابع (farqa' al-asabi'): Cracking or popping the finger joints, which is disliked in prayer.
التخصر (al-takhassur): Placing hands on the hips (like a posture of arrogance), disliked in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered a disliked action (makruh) in prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wiping sweat once with a sleeve.
B) Cracking fingers.
C) Adjusting the head covering at the beginning of prayer.
D) Closing eyes to increase concentration.

Key Takeaway
Abath, turning pebbles more than once, cracking fingers, and placing hands on hips are makruh tanzihi actions that reduce the reward of salah but do not invalidate it.

Three Actions That Do Not Invalidate Prayer: Looking, Eating Remnants, and Passing
Explaining what does not invalidate prayer and the sin of passing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that looking at writing, eating less than a chickpea from between teeth, and having someone pass in front do not invalidate prayer, and identify the conditions and sin involved.

Core Idea
Looking, eating a tiny leftover, and a passerby — each incurs potential sin but never breaks the prayer.

This lesson examines three common scenarios that students often think invalidate prayer. The Hanafi school teaches that the prayer remains valid (sahih) in all three, though the actor may be sinful in some cases. We will study the precise conditions, the evidence from the hadith, and the intra-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Name the three actions listed in the matn: (1) looking at a written text and understanding it, (2) eating what is between the teeth, (3) a person passing in front of the worshipper within the prostration area.
Each is addressed separately.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For all three, the matn says 'la' (it does not invalidate). Confirm the prayer remains valid (sahih) in each case.
The word 'la' here means the prayer is not invalidated, not merely disliked.
3
Examine the conditions
For eating: only if the amount is less than a chickpea and the food was already inside the mouth (not taken from outside). For passing: the passerby sins only if he passes within the prostration area (various definitions: place of prostration, where vision falls, fifty cubits, etc.).
If the food is taken from outside, even a sesame seed invalidates.
4
Evaluate the sin
The passerby incurs sin (ithm) based on the hadith, but the prayer of the one being passed remains valid. The one who looks or eats does not sin unless the looking is with desire or the eating is deliberate and large.
Hanafis distinguish between sin and invalidation.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Muhammad ibn al-Hasan holds that looking at non-Quranic writing invalidates the prayer; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it does not. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority view.
This shows the importance of tarjih within the school.

Source Text

Book of Prayer, Chapter of What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked in It. And if he looked at writing and understood it, or ate what was between his teeth, or a passerby passed in the place of his prostration, it does not [invalidate], even if he sins. And the prayer of the passerby in the third [issue]—meaning the one praying. What reaches the size of a chickpea or more [invalidates if swallowed], not what is less than that, unless he takes it from outside, then it invalidates even if the size of a sesame seed. And remaining of the invalidators: death, apostasy in the heart, insanity, fainting, and everything that necessitates wudu or ghusl, and leaving a pillar without making it up, or a condition without an excuse, and the mistake of the reciter. And it is narrated from Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar that if the prayer is valid in one respect and invalid in another, it is judged invalid as a precaution, except in the chapter of recitation because people have a general affliction in it. And in al-Mudmarat: He recited in prayer with a gross mistake, then repeated and recited correctly, his prayer is valid. (Ayni, Fath) And drinking intentionally or forgetfully, little or much, because it is excessive action. (Ayni) And (Ta) to writing: before him or on the wall, and there is a difference from Muhammad if the writing is other than Quran. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/74) His teeth: what is less than a chickpea because it is impossible to avoid it. (Ayni) In the place of his prostration: because he only sins if he passes in the place of his prostration according to the most correct view, and it is said: in the place where his vision would fall if he prayed with humility and submissiveness, and it is said: fifty cubits, and it is said: the extent of two rows. This if the place of prayer and passage are one. But if the one praying is on a bench and the passerby passes on the ground and the bench is like the height of a man, there is no harm in the desert. And in the mosque, if there is a barrier between them, it is not disliked; and if there is no barrier and the mosque is small, i.e., less than forty cubits, it is disliked wherever it is, and it is said: three cubits, and it is said: the extent of two or three rows. And the passerby sins because of his statement: "If the passerby in front of the one praying knew what burden is upon him, he would stop for forty." (Fath) No: i.e., his prayer is not invalidated in the three issues. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses three specific actions that do not invalidate prayer but may involve sin. First, looking at writing and understanding its content does not break the prayer. Second, eating what remains between teeth is also non-invalidating if the amount is less than a chickpea, provided it was not taken from outside the mouth; if taken from outside, even a tiny amount invalidates because it is an intentional extraneous act. Third, a person passing directly in front of the one praying does not invalidate the prayer, but the passerby commits a sin. The commentary explains that the sin applies only when passing within the prostration area, with varying definitions. The hadith cited warns of the severe burden on the passerby. The passage also lists other major invalidators briefly, such as death, apostasy, and excessive movement. The notes clarify that Muhammad ibn al-Hasan differed regarding looking at non-Quranic writing, but the relied-upon view is that it does not invalidate. The overall ruling is that the prayer remains valid (sahih) in these cases.

Key Terms
الحمصة (al-himmassah): A chickpea; used as a measure for the amount of food that invalidates prayer if eaten intentionally and remembered.
المار (al-marr): A person who passes between the praying person and the qibla, specifically within the prostration area.
الوزر (al-wizr): Sin or burden; in this context, the sin incurred by the passerby.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a person's prayer if someone passes in front of them while they are praying?
A) The prayer becomes invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer remains valid, but the passerby sins.
C) The prayer becomes makruh but is still valid.
D) The prayer becomes invalid only if the passerby is aware.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the prayer remains valid even if one looks at writing, eats a small leftover from between teeth, or has someone pass in front; only the passerby bears sin.

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Categories and Proofs
Listing and explaining disliked acts in prayer with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts in prayer are makruh tahrimi vs. makruh tanzihi, and explain the difference using Hanafi proofs.

Core Idea
Most disliked acts reduce reward but do not invalidate; only excessive movements or turning the chest invalidate.

Many actions in prayer, though not invalidating, diminish its perfection. The Hanafi school distinguishes between prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) and lightly disliked (makruh tanzihi) acts. This lesson examines a range of such acts from the matn and their proofs, allowing you to recognize both the ruling and its gradation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Recognize that the prayer remains valid (sahih) when these acts are performed, but the reward is reduced unless the act is excessive enough to invalidate.
The overall hukm for most acts is karahah, not invalidating.
2
Categorize each disliked act
For each act (playing, pebble leveling, cracking fingers, etc.), note whether the text labels it makruh tahrimi, makruh tanzihi, or simply makruh without further specification.
Look for explicit terms like 'كراهته تحريمية' or 'كراهته تنزيهية'.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
Identify the hadith or other evidence cited for each act. For example, the prohibition of wiping pebbles is from the hadith of Mu'ayqib.
Some proofs are explicit quotations; others are referenced indirectly.
4
Apply the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the proof indicates karahah. For the hadith 'لا تمسح وأنت تصلي', the imperative prohibition (nahy) indicates karahah, and the concession 'فواحدة' shows that one wipe is excused, making more than that disliked.
Hanafi usul: a nahy from the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicates prohibition unless there is a contrary indicator.
5
Note exceptions and conditions
Identify cases where an act is not disliked (e.g., wiping sweat with purpose, leveling pebbles once) or becomes invalidating (e.g., scratching three times with hand lifting, turning the chest).
The text clarifies that 'playing' is disliked only without purpose; with purpose it is permissible.

وعقص شعره : [ هو أن يجمع الشعر على هامة ويشده بخيط أو بخرقة أو بصمغ؛ ليتلبد قبل الصلاة ثم يدخل فيها كذلك، ولو عقصه في الصلاة تفسد صلاته؛ لأنه عمل كثير. (مستخلص)
And his playing with his garment and body is disliked, and turning over pebbles except for prostration once, and cracking the fingers, and placing the hand on the hip—beginning of the disliked acts. And his playing is disliked without purpose; if it is with purpose like wiping sweat from his face, there is no harm. And said in al-Fayd: scratching with one hand in one pillar three times invalidates his prayer if he lifts his hand each time, otherwise no. (Fath) Except for prostration once: it is narrated in the six books from Mu‘ayqib that he عليه said: 'Do not wipe while you are praying; if you must, then once.' (Fath) Once means if the pebbles prevent him from prostrating, he may level them once and not increase. (Ayni) And cracking: i.e., pressing them and stretching them until they sound, and likewise interlacing them. (Ta) The fingers because of his statement: 'Do not crack your fingers,' and the ruling of interlacing is like cracking, due to the statement of Ibn [Mas‘ud] regarding interlacing the fingers: 'That is the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry,' and the Prophet ص saw a man interlacing his fingers in prayer and he separated his fingers. (Fath) And placing hand on hip: [it is placing the hand on the waist (Ta)] i.e., it is disliked with tahrimi karahah because of his statement: 'Placing the hand on the hip in prayer is the resting of the people of the Fire,' and the waist is what is between the head of the hip bone and the last rib in the side. (Fath) Book of Prayer, Chapter of What Invalidates Prayer and What is Disliked in It. And looking around, and sitting like a dog, and spreading his forearms, and returning the greeting with his hand, and sitting cross-legged without excuse—all are disliked. And gathering his hair, and folding his garment, and letting it hang—all are also disliked. And looking around is looking to the right and left, and the disliked form is to turn the neck until the face leaves the direction of the qibla. If he looks from the corner of his eye to the right or left without turning the neck, it is not disliked, though it is better to leave it; and turning with the chest is an invalidator. (Ta, Miskin) And iq‘a: [sitting like a dog (Ta)] i.e., to sit on the buttocks, erect the thighs, draw the knees to the chest, and place hands on the ground—this is the most correct. This is what is meant in the hadith of Abu Dharr: 'My friend forbade me from three things: pecking like a rooster, squatting like a dog, and spreading like a fox.' Its karahah is tahrimi. What is said in its interpretation: to erect his feet and sit on his heels placing his hands on the ground is also disliked, but its karahah is tanzihi. (Mustakhlas, Fath) His forearms: i.e., spreading them in prostration for a man; as for a woman, she should do that. (Miskin) And returning the greeting with his hand means with hand and head; with the tongue it is an absolute invalidator. There is no harm if the one praying answers with his head if asked for something or sees a dirham, etc. And if he shakes hands with the intention of greeting, his prayer is invalid. (Fath) And sitting cross-legged etc. [i.e., it is disliked because it abandons the sunnah of sitting for the tashahhud]—it is inserting the shins and thighs under each other, placing them on the ground to the right and left. It is disliked in prayer, not outside it, due to abandoning the sunnah of sitting. The explanation that it is the sitting of tyrants is not strong, for the Prophet عليه used to sit cross-legged in some situations, such that he was once eating cross-legged when revelation came: 'Eat as a servant eats,' and he was pure from the character of tyrants. Likewise the sitting of 'Umar in his mosque was cross-legged. The correct view is that sitting on the knees is closer to humility and is preferable in prayer except for an excuse. (Mustakhlas) And gathering his hair: [it is to collect the hair on the crown and tie it with a thread, cloth, or gum to mat it before prayer, then enter prayer like that; if he gathers it during prayer, it invalidates because it is excessive action. (Ayni)] It is said in its interpretation: to twist his locks around his head as women sometimes do. The karahah of gathering is because it is narrated that he forbade a man from praying with gathered hair. (Fath, Mustakhlas) And folding his garment: [i.e., raising it from front or back during prostration, because it is a type of arrogance. (Ayni)] Included in folding is rolling up the sleeves to the elbows. It is not disliked to wipe dust from the forehead in prayer; the correct view is that it is disliked unless there is harm. There is no harm after finishing before the greeting, but leaving it is better. The karahah of folding is due to the narration from Ibn 'Abbas: 'I was commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold hair or garments.' (Fath) And letting it hang: because it is reported that he forbade letting hang, which is placing the cloak on the head or shoulder and letting its ends trail, or putting the outer garment on the shoulder without inserting the arms into the sleeves. It is disliked whether there is a shirt underneath or not. (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage enumerates numerous disliked acts (makruhat) in prayer, each with its specific ruling and often a proof from hadith. The commentary clarifies degrees of karahah: some acts are makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), others makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked). For example, placing hands on the hips (takhassur) and the dog-like sitting (iq‘a) in its common form are makruh tahrimi, while another form of iq‘a is makruh tanzihi. Looking around with the chest invalidates, but with the eyes without turning the neck is not makruh. Returning the greeting with the hand is makruh; with the tongue it invalidates. Sitting cross-legged is makruh because it abandons the sunnah of sitting for tashahhud, though the hadith indicate the Prophet himself sat cross-legged outside prayer. Gathering hair, folding garments, and letting them hang are all makruh. The passage also notes that some acts, like wiping sweat or scratching once, are excused if necessary. The overall principle is that the prayer remains valid but with deficiency; only some severe acts invalidate.

Key Terms
المكروه (al-makruh): An act that is disliked in prayer; its omission is rewarded but its commission is not punished unless it is makruh tahrimi, which is close to haram.
الإقعاء (al-iq‘a’): A sitting posture resembling a dog, with buttocks on the ground, thighs erect, knees drawn to chest, and hands on the ground. Considered makruh tahrimi.
الالتفات (al-iltifat): Turning the head to look to the right or left. If the chest turns, it invalidates; if only the neck turns, it is makruh tahrimi; if only the eyes, it is not disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on scratching the body three times with one hand while praying?
A) It invalidates the prayer because it is excessive action.
B) It is disliked but does not invalidate.
C) It invalidates only if the hand is lifted each time.
D) It is permissible without any dislike.

Key Takeaway
Disliked acts in prayer reduce reward but do not invalidate, except for excessive movements or turning the chest; always check the degree of karahah and the specific proofs.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Disliked Acts in Prayer: Turning, Squatting, Spreading, Responding, Cross-Legged Sitting
Lists disliked acts with definitions and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi for five specific disliked acts in prayer and state the correct Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Not every disliked action invalidates prayer; some are merely discouraged (tanzihi) while others are prohibitively disliked (tahrimi).

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā’iq enumerates actions that are disliked in prayer, clarifying their definitions and the degree of dislike. Understanding these distinctions is crucial because the ruling affects whether the prayer must be repeated (if tahrimi) or is valid but less virtuous (if tanzihi). We will examine five acts: turning the neck, squatting with hands on the floor, spreading forearms, responding to salaam with a gesture, and sitting cross-legged without excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each of the five acts, note the explicit ruling stated in the text: is it 'makruh' without further specification, 'makruh tanzihi', or 'makruh tahrimi'?
The text uses 'يكره' and sometimes qualifies with 'تحريمية' or 'تنزيهية'.
2
Analyze the definition and conditions
Read the definition carefully. For example, iltifat (turning) is only disliked if the neck is twisted; iq‘ā’ has two forms. Mark the boundary between disliked and invalidating (e.g., turning with the chest invalidates).
Note that some acts become invalidating (mufsid) if done in a certain way.
3
Examine the evidence (dalīl)
The passage cites a hadith for iq‘ā’ (Abu Dharr) and for kaff (Ibn Abbas). Understand how the hadith supports the ruling.
Evidence strengthens the ruling and shows it is not mere opinion.
4
Apply to a scenario
Imagine a person in prayer who performs one of these acts. Determine whether the prayer remains valid, needs repetition, or is merely lacking reward.
The practical consequence (thamarah) flows from the distinction.
5
Distinguish between similar cases
For instance, compare responding to salaam with hand (disliked) vs. with tongue (invalidates). Also compare iq‘ā’ with hands on floor (tahrimi) vs. on heels (tanzihi).
These nuances prevent overgeneralization.

كتاب الصلاة
باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها
والالتفات، والإقعاء، وافتراش ذراعيه ورد السلام بيده، والتربع بلا عذر، أي يكره
أي يكره أيضاً
وعقص شعره، وكف ثوبه، وسدله
أي يكره أيضا
أي ويكره أيضاً
والالتفات وهو النظر إلى اليمين والشمال والمكروه منه أن يلوي عنقه حتى يخرج وجهه من جهة القبلة، ولو نظر بمؤخر عينه يمنة أو يسرة بغير التلوية فلا يكره والأولى تركه وبالصدر مفسد. (ط، مسكين) والإقعاء [الجلوس مثل الكلب. (ط)] وهو أن يقعد على إليتيه وينصب فخذيه ويضم ركبتيه إلى صدره ويضع يديه على الأرض، وهو الأصح ، وهذا هو المراد في حديث أبي ذر نهاني خليلي عن ثلاث: أن أنقر كنقر الديك، وأن أقعي كإقعاء الكلب، وأن أفترش كافتراش الثعلب"، وكراهته تحريمية، وما قيل في تفسيره: أن ينصب قدميه ويقعد على عقبيه واضعاً يديه على الأرض فمكروه أيضاً، لكن كراهته تنزيهية. (مستخلص، فتح) ذراعيه: أي بسطهما في السجود للرجل، أما المرأة فينبغي لها ذلك. (مسكين) ورد السلام بيده أي باليد والرأس، وباللسان مفسد مطلقاً، ولا بأس بإجابة المصلي برأسه كما لو طلب منه شيء أو رأى درهماً، وقيل: "أجيد"؟ فأوماً برأسه بـ"نعم" أو "لا"، أو قيل: "كم صليتم"؟ فأشار بيده أنهم صلوا ركعتين، ولو صافح بنية السلام تفسد صلاته. (فتح) والتربع إلخ [ أي يكره؛ لأن فيه ترك سنة القعود للتشهد] وهو إدخال الساقين والفخذين بعضها تحت بعض، ووضعهما على الأرض يميناً وشمالاً، وكره في الصلاة فلا يكره خارجها لترك سنة القعود فيها، والتعليل بأنه جلوس الجبابرة ليس بقوي؛ فإن النبي علي كان يتربع في جلوسه في بعض أحواله، حتى أنه كان يأكل يوماً متربعاً فنزل عليه الوحي، كل" كما يأكل العبد، وهو كان منزها من أخلاق الجبابرة، وكذلك كان جلوس عمر له في مسجده عليه متربعا، والصحيح أن الجلوس على الركبتين أقرب إلى التواضع من التربع وهو أولى في حالة الصلاة إلا عند العذر. (مستخلص)
وعقص شعره : [ هو أن يجمع الشعر على هامة ويشده بخيط أو بخرقة أو بصمغ؛ ليتلبد قبل الصلاة ثم يدخل فيها كذلك، ولو عقصه في الصلاة تفسد صلاته؛ لأنه عمل كثير. (ع)] قيل في تفسيره: أن يلف ذوائبه حول رأسه كما تفعله النساء في بعض الأوقات وكراهة العقص لما روي أنه عليه فهى أن يصلي رجل وهو معقوص الشعر. (فتح مستخلص وكف ثوبه [ أي رفعه من بين يديه أو من خلفه عند السجود؛ لأنه نوع تجبر. (ع)] ويدخل في الكف تشمير كميه إلى المرفقين، ولا يكره مسح جبهته من التراب في الصلاة، والصحيح أنه يكره إلا للأذى، ولا بأس به بعد الفراغ قبل السلام والترك أفضل وكراهة الكف؛ لما روي عن ابن عباس لما أن . قال: "أمرت أن أسجد على سبع ولا أكف الشعر ولا الثياب فتح وغيره وسدله: لما ورد أنه علية نهى عن السدل، وهو أن يضع الرداء على رأسه أو كتفه ويرسل أطرافه أو يجعل القباء على الكتف ولم يدخل يديه في الكمين، وهو مكروه، سواء كان تحته قميص أو لا. (مستخلص)
رسول .
الله
The Book of Prayer. Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked therein. Turning away, squatting, spreading forearms, responding to salam with hand, sitting cross-legged without excuse—all are disliked. Also disliked: knotting hair, folding garment, and letting it hang. Turning away is looking to right and left; the disliked form is to twist the neck so that the face leaves the direction of the qibla. If one looks with the corner of the eye without twisting, it is not disliked but leaving it is better; turning with the chest invalidates. (T, Miskin) Iq‘ā’ is sitting like a dog: sitting on the buttocks, extending thighs, drawing knees to chest, placing hands on the ground—this is the soundest view. This is meant in the hadith of Abu Dharr: 'My beloved forbade me from three: pecking like a rooster, squatting like a dog, and spreading like a fox.' Its dislike is tahrimi (prohibitive). What is said in its interpretation—that one extends feet and sits on heels with hands on the ground—is also disliked but its dislike is tanzihi (advisory). (Mustakhlas, Fath) Spreading forearms means spreading them in prostration for a man; for a woman, it is commendable to do so. (Miskin) Responding to salam with hand means with hand and head; responding with tongue invalidates absolutely. It is acceptable for the praying person to respond with his head to something requested or if he sees a dirham, or if asked 'Is it good?' and he indicates 'yes' or 'no' with his head, or if asked 'How many did you pray?' and he indicates with hand that they prayed two rak‘ahs. If he shakes hands with the intention of greeting, his prayer becomes invalid. (Fath) Sitting cross-legged etc. [is disliked because it abandons the sunnah of sitting for tashahhud] — it is to insert legs and thighs under each other, placing them on the ground right and left. It is disliked in prayer but not outside it due to abandoning the sunnah of sitting therein. The explanation that it is the sitting of tyrants is not strong, for the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to sit cross-legged in some states; indeed, he was eating one day cross-legged when revelation came: 'Eat as a servant eats.' And he was innocent of the morals of tyrants. Likewise, ‘Umar used to sit cross-legged in his mosque. The correct view is that sitting on the knees is closer to humility than sitting cross-legged and is preferable in prayer except when there is an excuse. (Mustakhlas) Knotting hair is to gather the hair on the crown and tie it with thread, cloth, or glue to mat it before prayer, then enter prayer thus. If he knots it during prayer, his prayer becomes invalid due to excessive action. (Ayni) An interpretation: to wrap the locks around the head as women do sometimes. Its dislike is based on the report that he (peace be upon him) forbade a man praying while his hair was knotted. (Fath, Mustakhlas) Folding the garment is to raise it from the front or back during prostration, as it is a type of arrogance. It includes tucking sleeves to the elbows. It is not disliked to wipe the forehead from dust during prayer; the correct view is that it is disliked unless for harm/trouble. It is acceptable after finishing before salam, but leaving it is better. Its dislike is based on the report from Ibn ‘Abbas: 'I was commanded to prostrate on seven [bones] and not to fold back hair or garments.' (Fath and others) Letting it hang: it is reported that he (peace be upon him) forbade letting hang, which is to place the cloak on the head or shoulder and let its ends hang, or to place the coat on the shoulder without inserting hands into sleeves. It is disliked, whether there is a shirt underneath or not. (Mustakhlas) Rasul Allah.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is a ruling_statement from Kanz al-Daqā’iq with commentary. It enumerates several disliked acts in prayer, defining each and clarifying their degree of dislike. The commentator follows the order of the matn, expanding on each act with hadith evidence and legal reasoning. For example, turning away is only disliked if the neck is twisted; otherwise, it is not. The squatting (iq‘ā’) has two forms: one with hands on ground (tahrimi dislike) and one on heels (tanzihi). Responding to salam with hand is disliked, but with tongue invalidates prayer. Sitting cross-legged is disliked because it abandons the sunnah sitting; however, the Prophet himself sometimes sat cross-legged outside prayer, so the ruling is specific to prayer. Knotting hair before prayer is disliked, but if done during prayer it invalidates due to excessive action. Folding garments and letting them hang are disliked based on prophetic prohibitions. The text consistently distinguishes between makruh tahrimi and tanzihi where applicable.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifāt): Looking to the right or left by turning the neck so the face leaves the qibla direction.
الإقعاء (al-iq‘ā’): Sitting on the buttocks with thighs extended, knees drawn to chest, and hands on the ground (like a dog).
افتراش ذراعيه (iftirāsh al-dhirā‘ayn): Spreading the forearms flat on the ground during prostration, like a beast.
رد السلام بيده (radd al-salām bi-yadihi): Responding to the greeting of peace with a hand gesture or head nod, not verbally.
التربع (al-tarabbuh): Sitting cross-legged with legs folded under each other, placed on the ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following actions is considered makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) in prayer?
A) Looking to the left with the corner of the eye
B) Squatting (iq‘ā’) by sitting on the buttocks with hands on the ground
C) Spreading forearms in prostration for a man
D) Responding to salaam with a hand gesture

Key Takeaway
A clear understanding of the difference between makruh tahrimi and tanzihi is essential to know whether a disliked act in prayer requires repetition or is merely a loss of reward.

Salah — Prayer

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Hair Knotting, Folding Garment, Letting Hang
Lists disliked acts with definitions and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rulings for knotting hair, folding the garment, and letting the garment hang in prayer, and distinguish between actions disliked before vs. during prayer.

Core Idea
Knotting hair and folding garments in prayer are disliked because they contradict the prophetic command to prostrate with natural hair and loose garments.

This passage continues the enumeration of disliked acts in prayer, focusing on three actions related to grooming and clothing: knotting hair (عقص), folding the garment (كف), and letting the garment hang loosely (سدل). For each, the text provides definition, evidence, and the consequence if done during prayer (e.g., knotting during prayer invalidates due to excessive action).

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of each act
For 'aqs: gathering hair on the crown and tying it with thread, cloth, or glue before prayer. For kaff: raising the front or back of the garment during prostration. For sadl: placing a cloak on head/shoulder without inserting arms.
Note the difference between doing the action before prayer (disliked) vs. during prayer (may invalidate for 'aqs).
2
Identify the evidence (dalīl)
For 'aqs: hadith forbidding a man praying with knotted hair. For kaff: hadith of Ibn Abbas about not folding back clothes. For sadl: prophetic prohibition.
The hadith provide quranic or sunnah basis for the dislike.
3
Distinguish the ruling based on timing
Knotting hair before prayer: disliked. Knotting during prayer: invalidates due to excessive action. Folding garment: disliked (makruh) as it resembles arrogance. Letting hang: disliked alike.
This timing distinction is unique to 'aqs.
4
Apply to practical scenarios
For example, a man praying with his sleeves rolled up to his elbows: this is included in kaff (folding). Similarly, a woman who let her hair hang loosely is not disliked; only knotting it up is.

The commentator follows the order of the matn, expanding on each act with hadith evidence and legal reasoning. For example, turning away is only disliked if the neck is twisted; otherwise, it is not. The squatting (iq‘ā’) has two forms: one with hands on ground (tahrimi dislike) and one on heels (tanzihi). Responding to salam with hand is disliked, but with tongue invalidates prayer. Sitting cross-legged is disliked because it abandons the sunnah sitting; however, the Prophet himself sometimes sat cross-legged outside prayer, so the ruling is specific to prayer. Knotting hair before prayer is disliked, but if done during prayer it invalidates due to excessive action. Folding garments and letting them hang are disliked based on prophetic prohibitions. The text consistently distinguishes between makruh tahrimi and tanzihi where applicable.

Key Terms
عقص الشعر (‘aqṣ al-sha‘r): Gathering and tying hair on the head with thread, cloth, or glue to keep it matted during prayer.
كف الثوب (kaff al-thawb): Folding or tucking the garment, especially raising it from the front or back during prostration.
السدل (al-sadl): Placing a cloak or garment over the head or shoulders without inserting arms into sleeves, letting it hang loosely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a person knots his hair (عقص) during the prayer, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalid because it is excessive action.
B) The prayer is valid but the action is disliked.
C) The prayer is valid and the action is permissible.
D) The prayer becomes invalid only if the hair was long.

Key Takeaway
Knotting hair during prayer invalidates due to excessive action, but doing so before prayer is only disliked; folding garments and letting them hang are disliked based on prophetic prohibitions.

Disliked Acts in Prayer (Part 2)
Expansion on disliked acts with rulings and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on yawning, closing eyes, imam standing in the mihrab, standing on a raised platform, and wearing garments with images during prayer, and identify the exceptions and underlying reasoning.

Core Idea
Many actions are disliked (makruh) in prayer but do not invalidate; necessity can lift the dislike.

This passage expands on the Kanz al-Daqa'iq list of disliked acts in prayer, covering yawning, closing eyes, the imam's position in the mihrab, standing on a platform, and wearing garments with images. Each act is addressed with its ruling, evidence, and conditions for exception. Understanding these helps maintain the ideal state of prayer while knowing when allowances apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked act and its ruling
List the actions mentioned: yawning, closing eyes, imam standing in mihrab, imam alone on platform (dukkān), congregation alone on platform, wearing garments with images. Note the ruling: makruh tanzihan (disliked) for all, except making images which is haram.
The text also mentions stretching, praying while neediing to relieve oneself, and fanning as disliked.
2
Locate the dalil for each act
Find the proof cited: for yawning, the hadith 'Allah loves sneezing and dislikes yawning'; for closing eyes, a prohibition reported; for mihrab, resemblance to People of the Book; for platform, disdain for imam; for images, hadith on picture-makers and Jibril not entering a house with images.
Some proofs are direct sunnah, others are derived from general principles like avoiding resemblance.
3
Note exceptions and excuses
Identify when the ruling changes: yawning may require covering mouth if cannot suppress; imam in mihrab is allowed if mosque is crowded; platform standing is allowed for teaching or if space is tight; images on garments are disliked but not haram if worn inadvertently.
The principle is that dislike is removed by necessity or valid need.
4
Understand the reasoning behind each ruling
Reflect on the reasons: yawning is from laziness and disrupts focus; closing eyes abandons sunnah; mihrab resembles non-Muslims; platform creates disdain; images resemble idolatry.
These reasons help in analogizing to new situations.

الأبد. (فتح)
Yawning, closing eyes, the imam standing (but not prostrating) in the niche, the imam being alone on the platform and its opposite, wearing a garment with images—all are disliked. Yawning is from laziness and fullness and disturbs focus; it is disliked in and out of prayer. The Prophet said: 'Allah loves sneezing and dislikes yawning.' If overcome by yawning, one should suppress it as much as possible, even by biting the lip if possible, otherwise by hand. If one covers his mouth with his hand while able to bite his lip, it is disliked because covering is disliked except for necessity. Similarly, stretching is disliked due to laziness. (Fath) Closing the eyes: because the sunnah is to fix the gaze on the place of prostration, and closing abandons this sunnah; also every limb has a share in worship, so the eye. It is also disliked to pray while needing to relieve oneself of the two impurities or one of them or wind. If it distracts him, he should cut prayer if there is ample time. It is disliked to fan oneself with a fan or sleeve, but it does not invalidate unless excessive. (Fath) It is reported that he (peace be upon him) forbade closing eyes in prayer. (Mustakhlas) The imam standing in the niche (mihrab) is disliked because it resembles the People of the Book. Not his prostration if he is standing outside the niche; what matters is the foot. (Ramz al-Haqā’iq 1:75) In the niche: due to resemblance to People of the Book or confusion of his state for those on right and left; the People of the Book allocate a high place for the imam. This dislike applies when there is no excuse; it is reported from al-Tajnīs that if the mosque is tight for those behind him, there is no harm in standing in the niche. (Fath, Miskin) Al-dukkān is a raised place about a man's height; it is said about a cubit, which is sound; what is less than that is fine. (Ayni) Its opposite means the congregation standing on the platform alone, because it shows disdain for the imam. If some of them are with him, it is not disliked in the sound view. (Ayni) This applies when there is no excuse like Friday or Eid. If they stand while the imam is on the ground or in the mihrab due to narrow space, it is not disliked. Among excuses is teaching or conveying. Standing behind a row that has a gap is disliked due to prohibition. If one cannot find a gap, he may pull someone from the row, but they said in our time: leaving it is better. It is disliked for a person to allocate a specific spot in the mosque for prayer, because prayer in that spot becomes habitual, and worship when habitual is abandoned; thus fasting is disliked. Wearing a garment with images of living beings is disliked because it resembles carrying an idol. (Ayni, T) Images on a garment are disliked whether one prays or not. Al-Thawri mentioned consensus on the prohibition of drawing pictures of animals; it is severely unlawful and a major sin. The Prophet said: 'The most severely punished people on the Day of Resurrection are the picture-makers; they will be told: Give life to what you have created.' And it is reported that Jibril (peace be upon him) made an appointment with the Prophet but delayed until it became hard on him; the Prophet went out and met him, and he said: 'We do not enter a house that has a dog or a picture.' (Fath) Forever. (Fath)
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This continues the ruling_statement from the same chapter, discussing additional disliked acts. Yawning is disliked, and one should suppress it by biting the lip or covering the mouth only if necessary. Closing the eyes abandons the sunnah of looking at the prostration place. The imam standing in the mihrab is disliked due to resemblance to non-Muslims, but if the mosque is crowded, it becomes permissible. The imam or congregation standing on a raised platform (dukkān) is disliked if it shows disdain, but permitted for teaching or if space is tight. Finally, wearing garments with images of living beings is disliked, and creating such images is a major sin. The text integrates hadith evidence and scholarly consensus.

Key Terms
التثاؤب (al-tathā’ub): Yawning, considered from laziness and disliked in prayer and generally.
تغميض العينين (taghmīḍ al-‘aynayn): Closing the eyes during prayer, abandoning the sunnah of looking at the prostration place.
الطاق (al-ṭāq): The niche or mihrab in the mosque where the imam stands.
الدكان (al-dukkān): A raised platform, about the height of a man or cubit, where the imam or congregation stands.
تصاوير (taṣāwīr): Images or pictures of living beings (animals, humans) on garments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is disliked (makruh) because it shows disdain for the imam.
C) It is permissible (ja'iz) without any dislike.
D) It invalidates the prayer.

Key Takeaway
Disliked acts in prayer are makruh tanzihi and do not invalidate, but they should be avoided unless necessity provides an excuse.

Ruling on Yawning and Stretching in Prayer
Explains disliked nature of yawning and stretching with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on yawning and stretching in prayer, identify the hadith proof, and explain the proper method of suppression.

Core Idea
Yawning and stretching in prayer are disliked; suppress the yawn by taking the lip, not by covering the mouth unnecessarily.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on two common involuntary actions during prayer: yawning and stretching. Although they may seem minor, the school classifies them as makruh (disliked) due to their association with laziness and disturbance of concentration. The source text provides the hadith proof and details the preferred method of suppression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Clearly state that yawning and stretching in and out of prayer are both makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful).
The ruling is established from the hadith.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Indeed Allah loves sneezing and dislikes yawning' (recorded in Bukhari and Muslim).
This is a hadith ahad, but accepted for rulings of karahah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'yakrahu' (dislikes) indicates karahah (dislike), not tahrim (prohibition), establishing makruh tanzih.
The hadith does not say 'forbids', so the level is dislike, not sin.
4
Analyze the suppression method
If yawning overcomes, one must suppress it. The preferred method is taking the lower lip with the front teeth. If unable, cover with hand. But covering unnecessarily is itself disliked.
The text from Al-Fath provides this nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتثاؤب [لأنه من الكسل والامتلاء ويخلّ بالتوجه، وهو مكروه في الصلاة وخارجها. (ط)] أي مطلقاً، لقوله عليه: "إن الله يحب العطاس ويكره التثاؤب"، فإن غلبه التثاؤب فليكظم ما استطاع ولو بأخذ شفته بسنة إن أمكنه وإلا فبيده حتى لو غطى فمه بيده متمكناً من أخذ شفته كره؛ لأن التغطية مكروهة إلا لضرورة، النبي . وكذا يكره التمطي أيضاً؛ لأنه من الكسل. (فتح)
And yawning [because it comes from laziness and fullness and it disturbs concentration, and it is disliked in prayer and outside it. (T)] meaning absolutely, due to his saying (peace be upon him): "Indeed Allah loves sneezing and dislikes yawning." So if yawning overcomes him, let him suppress it as much as possible, even by taking his lip with his front teeth if possible, otherwise with his hand; so much so that if he covers his mouth with his hand while being able to take his lip, it is disliked, because covering is disliked except due to necessity. And similarly stretching is also disliked, because it is from laziness. (Fath)
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on yawning and stretching during prayer. The author classifies both as makruh (disliked), whether inside or outside prayer, because they stem from laziness or fullness and disturb the proper attentiveness (tawajjuh) required in worship. The proof is a hadith stating that Allah loves sneezing and dislikes yawning. The passage then details how one should handle yawning if it overcomes him: he must suppress it as much as possible, preferably by placing his lip between his front teeth, or if unable, with his hand. However, if he covers his mouth with his hand while capable of taking the lip, it becomes disliked, as covering the mouth is generally discouraged except for necessity. Stretching (tamatti) is also disliked for the same reason—laziness.

Key Terms
التثاؤب (al-tathā'ub): Yawning, an involuntary opening of the mouth due to fatigue or drowsiness.
التمطي (al-tamatti): Stretching the body, especially the arms, as a sign of laziness.
كظم (kazm): Suppressing or holding back the yawn by closing the mouth or covering it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is makruh tanzih (disliked).
C) It is permissible without any dislike.
D) It is sunnah to yawn loudly.

Key Takeaway
Yawning and stretching in prayer are makruh tanzih; if yawning overcomes, suppress by taking the lip with teeth, not covering the mouth unnecessarily.

Disliked Actions in Prayer: Closing Eyes, Suppressing Urine, Fanning
Lists disliked actions and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on closing eyes, suppressing urine/stool, and fanning during prayer, and identify when these actions become prohibited or invalidate the prayer.

Core Idea
Three disliked actions in prayer are makruh tanzih; only excessive fanning invalidates.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq lists three actions that are makruh (disliked) during prayer: closing the eyes, suppressing urine or stool, and fanning oneself. Understanding these rulings helps the Muslim maintain proper adab and concentration in prayer, and know the limits beyond which the prayer becomes invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions and their ruling
List each action and state the ruling (makruh tanzih) from the passage.
All three are disliked, but the degree of dislike may vary.
2
Locate the dalil for closing eyes
The passage cites a hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade closing eyes in prayer. Identify the source and how it proves the ruling.
The hadith is from Mustakhlas; its chain is not discussed.
3
Understand the reasoning for closing eyes
The sunnah is to look at the prostration spot; closing eyes abandons this sunnah. Also, every limb has a share in worship.
The eye's 'share' is to gaze at the place of sujud.
4
Apply the rule of excessive movement to fanning
Fanning is disliked but does not invalidate unless it becomes excessive (kathīr).
Excessive movement generally nullifies prayer if it is not part of the prayer.
5
Note the exception for suppressing urges
If suppressing urine/stool distracts the worshipper, it is mustahab to break the prayer if there is ample time.
This is because khushu' is essential, and the need may preoccupy the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و تغميض عينيه لأن السنة أن من يرمي بصره إلى موضع السجود، وفي التغميض ترك هذه السنة؛ ولأن كل عضو ذو حظ من العبادة فكذا العين، ويكره أيضاً أن يصلي وهو يدافع الأخبثين أو أحدهما أو الريح، فإن شغله قطعها حيث كان في الوقت سعة، ويكره أن يروح على نفسه بمروحة أو بكمه ولا تفسد إلا أن يكثر. (فتح) عينيه: روي أن علية نهى عن تغميض العين في الصلاة. (مستخلص)
And closing his eyes, because it is sunnah for a person to direct his gaze to the place of prostration, and in closing them is abandoning this sunnah; and because every limb has a share in worship, so too the eye. And it is also disliked that he pray while suppressing the two filths (urine and stool) or one of them or wind, for if it distracts him, he should break it (the prayer) if there is ample time. And it is disliked that he fan himself with a fan or his sleeve, and it does not invalidate unless it becomes excessive. Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage covers three separate disliked actions in prayer. First, closing the eyes: the sunnah is to look at the place of prostration, so closing the eyes abandons this sunnah. Additionally, every limb has its role in worship, and the eye's role is to look towards the prostration spot. A hadith explicitly forbids closing the eyes in prayer, establishing its disliked nature. Second, praying while suppressing urine, stool, or passing wind is disliked because it prevents proper concentration (khushu'). If the suppression distracts him fully, he should break the prayer if he still has ample time to perform it later. Third, fanning oneself with a fan or sleeve is disliked, but it does not invalidate the prayer unless the movement becomes excessive (i.e., many consecutive movements).

Key Terms
تغميض العينين (taghmīḍ al-ʿaynayn): Closing the eyes deliberately during prayer.
يدافع الأخبثين (yudāfiʿ al-akhbathayn): Suppressing urine and stool (or one of them) while praying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is disliked but does not invalidate unless excessive.
C) It is recommended to keep cool.
D) It is permissible without any dislike.

Key Takeaway
Three disliked actions in prayer—closing eyes, suppressing body functions, and fanning—are makruh tanzih; only excessive fanning invalidates, and suppressing urges may require breaking prayer if it distracts.

Ruling on the Imam in the Mihrab and Congregation on a Raised Platform
Ruling with proof from the principle of avoiding imitation of non-Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the imam standing in the mihrab and the congregation standing on a raised platform, and identify the exceptions based on valid excuses.

Core Idea
Imam in mihrab and congregation on dakkān are makruh unless there is a valid excuse.

This lesson examines the rulings related to the imam's position in the mosque and the congregation's arrangement. The Hanafi school considers it disliked (makruh tanzih) for the imam to stand inside the mihrab or for the entire congregation to stand on a raised platform, primarily due to imitation of non-Muslims or disrespect. However, these rulings are flexible when necessity arises, such as crowding or for educational purposes. Understanding these nuances helps in properly organizing congregational prayer while avoiding unnecessary dislike.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on imam in mihrab
Understand that it is makruh tanzih for the imam to stand inside the mihrab (arch/niche) because it resembles the practice of the People of the Book who elevated their leaders.
The ruling applies when there is no excuse; it is not sinful if avoided.
2
Recognize the exception for necessity
When the mosque is crowded or there is another valid excuse (e.g., teaching, delivering sermon), the dislike is lifted and it becomes permissible.
This is based on the principle that necessity overrides prohibition.
3
Analyze the ruling on congregation on dakkān
It is makruh for the entire congregation to stand on a raised platform (dakkān) while the imam is on the ground, as this shows contempt. However, if some people are with the imam, it is not disliked.
The correct view is that the platform must be at least a cubit high; less than that is not disliked.
4
Note additional rulings on rows and reserved spots
Standing behind a row with a gap is disliked; if no gap exists, one may pull someone forward but it is better to leave it. Also, reserving a specific spot for prayer is disliked as it leads to habit.
These rulings aim to maintain proper etiquette and sincerity in worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيام الإمام أي في الطاق وهو المحراب؛ لما فيه من التشبه بأهل الكتاب. لا سجوده: إذا كان قائماً خارج المحراب، والمعتبر هو القدم. [رمز الحقائق: ٧٥/١] في الطاق: لما فيه من التشبه بأهل الكتاب أو لاشتباه حاله على أهل اليمين واليسار، وأهل الكتاب يخصون الإمام بالمكان المرتفع، وهذه الكراهة عند عدم العذر؛ ولهذا نقل عن "التجنيس": أنه لو ضاق المسجد بمن خلفه لا بأس بقيامه في الطاق. (فتح مسكين الدكان هو الموضع المرتفع قدر قامة، وقيل: قدر الذراع، وهو الصحيح، ولا بأس بما دونها. (ع) وعكسه [ أي قيام القوم على الدكان فقط؛ لما فيه من الازدراء بالإمام، وإذا كان معه بعضهم لا يكره في الصحيح. (ع)] أي عند عدم العذر كجمعة وعيد، فلو قاموا على الوقوف والإمام على الأرض أو في المحراب لضيق المقام لا يكره، كما لو كان معه بعض القوم في الأصح، ومن العذر إرادة التعليم أو التبليغ، وكره القيام خلف صف فيه فرجة للنهي، وإن لم يجد فرجة يجذب أحداً من الصف لكن قالوا في زماننا: تركه أولى، ويكره للإنسان أن يخص نفسه بمكان في المسجد يصلي فيه؛ لأن الصلاة في ذلك المكان تصير طبعاً له والعبادة متى صارت طبعاً تترك؛ ولهذا كره صوم
And the imam's standing in the arch (tāq), which is the mihrab, because it contains imitation of the People of the Book. Not his prostration: when he is standing outside the mihrab, what matters is the foot. In the arch: because of imitation of the People of the Book or because his condition becomes unclear to those on the right and left, and the People of the Book used to single out the imam for a high place. This dislike is when there is no excuse; hence it is transmitted from al-Tajnīs: if the mosque becomes tight for those behind him, there is no harm in his standing in the arch. (Fath) The dakkān (raised platform) is a place raised the height of a qāmah, or some say a cubit, which is the correct view, and there is no harm in less than that. (Ayn) And its opposite [i.e., the people standing on the dakkān alone, because it contains contempt for the imam; and if some of them are with him, it is not disliked in the correct view. (Ayn)] i.e., when there is no excuse like Friday or Eid. So if they stand while the imam is on the ground or in the mihrab due to narrowness of the place, it is not disliked, just as if some of the people are with him in the more correct view. Among excuses are intending teaching or conveying (the message). And it is disliked to stand behind a row that has a gap, due to the prohibition. And if he does not find a gap, he may pull someone from the row, but they said in our time: leaving it is better. And it is disliked for a person to assign a specific place for himself in the mosque to pray in, because praying in that place becomes a habit, and when worship becomes a habit, it is abandoned; hence fasting (in a particular manner) is disliked.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings regarding the imam's position and the congregation's arrangement. It is disliked (makruh) for the imam to stand inside the mihrab (arched niche) because it resembles the practice of the People of the Book (Christians and Jews) who would place their leader in an elevated spot. However, this dislike applies only when there is no excuse; if the mosque is crowded, it is permissible. Similarly, it is disliked for the entire congregation to stand on a raised platform (dakkān) while the imam is on the ground, as this shows disrespect. If some of the congregation are with the imam on the ground, it is not disliked. Exceptions include Friday and Eid prayers when space is tight, or for the purpose of teaching or delivering the sermon. The passage then mentions that it is disliked to stand behind a row with a gap; if no gap exists, pulling someone forward is allowed, but leaving it is better. Finally, it is disliked to reserve a specific spot in the mosque for personal prayer, as this may lead to habit and neglect of worship.

Key Terms
الطاق (al-ṭāq): The arch or niche in the mosque, i.e., the mihrab.
الدكان (al-dakkān): A raised platform or seat in the mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition is it permissible for the imam to stand in the mihrab?
A) Always permissible.
B) When there is no excuse.
C) When the mosque is crowded or there is a valid excuse.
D) Only during Eid prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules the imam in the mihrab and congregation on a raised platform as makruh tanzih, but necessity and valid excuses lift the dislike, reflecting the balance between avoiding imitation and accommodating practical needs.

The Prohibition of Drawing and Wearing Images of Living Beings
Ruling on drawing and wearing images with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on drawing animate beings and on wearing garments with such images, and identify the primary hadith evidence and consensus for this ruling.

Core Idea
Drawing animate beings is a major sin; wearing such images resembles idolatry.

This lesson examines the severe prohibition of creating images of living creatures. The Hanafi school, based on authentic hadith and reported consensus, considers this act among the major sins. We will also explore the ruling on wearing clothes bearing such images, which is disliked due to resemblance to carrying an idol.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Drawing animals (taswir) is haram; wearing clothes with such images is makruh.
The text uses the verb 'takrah' (dislike) for wearing images.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith: 'The most severely punished people... the picture-makers.' Also the hadith of Jibril about not entering houses with dogs or images.
These are ahad hadith but accepted as authentic.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The severe punishment indicates the act is a major sin (kabirah). The analogy of carrying an idol shows wearing images is makruh.
4
Note the ijma
Al-Thawri reports consensus (ijma) on the prohibition of drawing animals.
Ijma strengthens the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولبس ثوب فيه تصاوير الذوات الأرواح؛ لأنه يشبه حامل الصنم. (ع ، ط ) تكره التصاوير على الثوب صلى أم لم يصل، وذكر الثوري الإجماع على تحريم تصوير صورة الحيوان فإنه حرام شديد التحريم، وهو من الكبائر؛ لقوله عليه: "أشد الناس عذاباً يوم القيامة المصورون يقال لهم أحيوا ما خلقتم"، وورد: أن جبريل عليه السلام واعد النبي عليه السلام فراث عليه – أي أبطأ حتى شق عليه فخرج النبي عليه السلام فلقيه، فقال: "إنا لا ندخل بيتاً فيه كلب ولا صورة". (فتح) وأن يكون فوق رأسه أو بين يديه أو بحذائه صورة، إلا أن تكون صغيرةً أو مقطوعة الرأس أو لغير ذي روح، وعد الآي والتسبيح، لا قتل الحية والعقرب، أي تكون الصورة فوق رأسه أي في السقف مقتضاه عدم الكراهة إذا كانت تحت رجليه أو في محل جلوسه؛ لأنها مهانة، ولكن يكره كراهة جعل الصورة في البيت لخبر، لا تدخل الملائكة بيتاً فيه كلب ولا صورة وأشدها الكراهة أن تكون أمام مصلي ثم فوق رأسه ثم بحذائه ثم خلفه [تبيين الحقائق: ٤١٤/١] بين يديه بأن تكون معلقة أو موضوعة صغيرة: [ بحيث لا تبدو للناظر إلا بالتأمل؛ لأن الكراهة باعتبار العبادة والصغيرة لا تعبد عادة. (ع)] لما روي أن خاتم أبي هريرة كان عليه ذبابتان وخاتم دانيال كان عليه أسد ولبوة وبينهما رجل يلحسانه والمراد بالصغيرة أن تكون بحال لو كانت على الأرض والناظر واقف لا يرى تفاصيل أعضائها. (فتح) أو مقطوعة الرأس لأنها لا تعبد بدون الرأس. [رمز الحقائق: ٧٦/١] لغير ذي روح كالشجرة والثمرة والكواكب ونحوها ؛ لأنها لا تعبد. (ط)
And wearing a garment containing images of living beings, because it resembles carrying an idol. (Ayn, T) Images on a garment are disliked whether one prays or not, and al-Thawri mentioned consensus on the prohibition of making an image of an animal, for it is severely forbidden and among the major sins, due to his saying (peace be upon him): "The most severely punished people on the Day of Resurrection are the picture-makers; it will be said to them: Give life to what you have created." And it is reported that Jibril (peace be upon him) had promised to visit the Prophet (peace be upon him) but he delayed until it troubled him, so the Prophet went out and met him, whereupon he said: "We do not enter a house in which there is a dog or an image." (Fath) And that there be an image above his head, or in front of him, or beside him, unless it is small, or decapitated, or of a non-living thing. And counting verses and tasbih, not killing the snake and scorpion. Meaning the image above his head, i.e., in the ceiling, implies no dislike if it is under his feet or in his sitting place, because it is humiliated. But it is disliked as is the dislike of placing an image in the house due to the report: "Angels do not enter a house in which there is a dog or an image." The strongest dislike is when it is in front of the worshipper, then above his head, then beside him, then behind him. [Tabyīn al-Ḥaqā'iq: 1/414] In front of him: if it is hanging or placed small, such that it is not noticeable except with reflection, because the dislike is based on worship and a small image is not normally worshipped. (Ayn) It is narrated that the ring of Abū Hurayrah had two flies on it, and the ring of Daniel had a lion and a lioness with a man licking them between them. Small means that if it were on the ground and the observer standing, he would not see the details of its limbs. (Fath) Or decapitated, because it cannot be worshipped without a head. [Rāmz al-Ḥaqā'iq: 1/76] For non-living things like a tree, fruit, stars, etc., because they are not worshipped. (T)
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses the severe prohibition of wearing clothes bearing images of animate beings, likening it to carrying an idol. Drawing images of animals is a major sin (kabīrah) according to consensus, based on the hadith threatening painters with severe punishment. When such images are present in the prayer area, the ruling varies: images above the head, in front, or beside the worshipper are disliked, with the most severe being in front. Exceptions include images that are small (not seen clearly without effort), decapitated (headless), or of inanimate objects (trees, stars). Small images are exempt because they are not typically objects of worship. The presence of any image in the house may prevent angels from entering, as per the hadith about Jibril. The passage also brings examples from the companions' rings to illustrate small images.

Key Terms
تصاوير الذوات الأرواح (taṣāwīr al-dhawāt al-arwāḥ): Images of living beings (animals, humans).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on drawing a picture of a horse?
A) Makruh tanzihi
B) Haram (major sin)
C) Mubah
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
Drawing animate beings is a major sin (haram), and wearing such images is disliked (makruh) because it resembles carrying an idol.

Images in the Prayer Area: Exceptions and Degrees of Dislike
Detailed ruling on images in prayer area.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain which images in the prayer area are disliked and which are exempt, and rank the positions of an image by degree of karahah.

Core Idea
Images in the prayer area are disliked unless small, headless, or inanimate; the strongest dislike is for images in front of the worshipper.

This lesson focuses on the ruling of having images present in the area where one prays. While images of animate beings are generally disliked, there are important exceptions: small images whose details are not readily visible, headless images, and images of inanimate objects. Furthermore, the position of the image relative to the worshipper matters, with images in front being the most disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Having any image of an animate being in the prayer area, whether above, in front, or beside, is disliked (makruh).
This is based on the hadith of Jibril.
2
Learn the exceptions
Three exceptions: small (saghira), decapitated (maqtuta al-ra's), or inanimate (ghayr dhi ruh).
Smallness is defined relative to the viewer's ability to discern details.
3
Understand the rationale
The dislike is due to resemblance to worship; small or incomplete images are not usually objects of adoration.
Example of Abu Hurayra's ring with flies.
4
Rank the positions
Most disliked: in front; then above head; then to the side; then behind. Underfoot or beneath seating is exempt from dislike.
Because underfoot it is humiliated (muhana).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
